Your Google Shopping Ads Aren’t Showing (And How to Fix It)?";}s:7:"content";O:8:"stdClass":2:{s:8:"rendered";s:21017:"
<p><a href="https://webappick.com/how-to-set-up-shopping-ads-on-google/">Google Shopping ads</a> not showing is most commonly caused by product feed issues, account or policy problems (such as disapprovals or suspensions), overly restrictive targeting, low bids, or incorrect campaign settings like budgets, schedules, or date ranges.</p>



<p>To resolve this, make sure your Merchant Center account is in good standing, your product data is accurate and well-optimized (including titles and images), bids are competitive, and campaigns are configured correctly—while also allowing enough time for review and the learning phase.</p>



<p>If you’re dealing with <a href="https://webappick.com/google-shopping-ads-benefits/">Google Shopping ads</a> not showing, you’re likely staring at an account that looks fine on the surface—but isn’t delivering impressions, clicks, or visibility. This happens more often than most advertisers expect, and it usually starts without warning. One day ads are live, the next day they’re invisible, leaving you wondering why <a href="https://webappick.com/google-shopping-ads-tracking/">Google Shopping ads</a> are not showing when nothing obvious has changed.</p>



<p>The reality is that when <a href="https://webappick.com/complete-guide-of-google-shopping-ads/">Google Shopping ads</a> are not serving, it’s rarely caused by one single issue. Small problems inside your product feed, Merchant Center setup, bidding, or account signals tend to stack up quietly until Google no longer feels confident showing your products. That’s why <a href="https://webappick.com/how-to-find-the-best-performing-keywords-for-google-shopping-ads/">Google Shopping ads</a> not getting impressions is often the first symptom, not the root problem.</p>



<p>In this guide, we’ll break down every real reason Shopping Ads stop showing and explain how to fix each one using clear, practical steps—based on how <a href="https://www.wordstream.com/google-ads" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Google Ads</a> and <a href="https://www.bigcommerce.com/glossary/google-merchant-center/" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Google Merchant Center</a> actually work today.&nbsp;</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">How do product feed and data issues cause Shopping ads not to show?</h2>



<p>If there’s one place where things usually go wrong when <a href="https://webappick.com/google-shopping-ads-feed/">Google Shopping ads</a> not showing becomes a problem, it’s the product feed. Not bidding. Not budgets. Not even campaign settings. Almost every case where Google Shopping ads are not serving can be traced back to how Google reads, trusts, and understands your product data.</p>



<p>Google Shopping works entirely on confidence. If Google isn’t confident about what you’re selling, how much it costs, or whether the information is accurate, it simply stops showing your products. And the frustrating part is that this often happens quietly, without a dramatic error alert.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Missing or Incorrect Required Attributes</h3>



<p>Every product in your feed must include required details like ID, title, price, availability, condition, and image link. When even one of these is missing or formatted incorrectly, Google may technically “accept” the product but still avoid showing it. This is one of the most overlooked reasons Google Shopping ads not getting impressions, especially in large catalogs.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Price and Availability Mismatches</h3>



<p>Google constantly checks your landing pages against your feed. If the price or stock status on your website doesn’t exactly match what’s in the feed, even temporarily, your products can be restricted or silently deprioritized. This mismatch alone explains a huge number of cases where advertisers wonder why Google Shopping ads are not showing despite everything appearing active.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Low-Quality or Non-Compliant Images</h3>



<p>Images matter more than most people realize. Blurry photos, text overlays, watermarks, promotional badges, or placeholder images reduce trust instantly. When Google can’t clearly see the product, it’s far less likely to show it, leading to Google Shopping ads not serving even when no hard disapproval exists.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Weak or Generic Product Titles</h3>



<p>Your product title is how Google understands relevance. Titles like “Men’s Shoes” or “Wireless Headphones” are too vague to compete. When Google can’t clearly match your products to search intent, impressions drop first, then stop entirely. This is a silent but very common reason behind Google Shopping ads not getting impressions.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">How to Fix Feed Issues the Right Way</h3>



<p>Start inside Google Merchant Center and focus on Errors, not warnings. Errors block visibility. Make sure your website data matches your feed exactly, improve titles with real product details, and use clean, high-quality images that show only the product. Once Google trusts your data again, visibility usually returns faster than expected.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Why do policy violations and account-level restrictions stop ads from running?</h2>



<p>Sometimes, Google Shopping ads not showing has nothing to do with your feed quality or bidding strategy. Instead, the problem sits deeper—at the policy or account level—where Google quietly limits visibility without shutting your account down entirely. This is where many advertisers get stuck, because things look “approved,” yet Google Shopping ads are not serving in real auctions.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Product or Category Policy Violations</h3>



<p>Google has strict rules around what can and can’t be advertised through Shopping. Restricted products, misleading claims, unsupported medical or financial promises, or even aggressive promotional language can trigger limitations. What makes this tricky is that products may remain technically approved while still being heavily suppressed, leading to Google Shopping ads not getting impressions with no obvious red flags.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Website and Landing Page Trust Issues</h3>



<p>Your website is part of the review process, not just the product feed. Missing contact information, unclear refund policies, broken checkout flows, or slow-loading pages can reduce trust fast. When Google isn’t confident that users will have a safe experience, it limits exposure, which often explains why Google Shopping ads are not showing even when feeds look clean.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Account Suspensions and Soft Holds</h3>



<p>Not all suspensions look dramatic. In many cases, Google applies “soft” restrictions where ads technically remain enabled, but impressions collapse. Payment issues, billing disputes, repeated disapprovals, or previous policy violations can all contribute. This is one of the most frustrating reasons Google Shopping ads not serving, because nothing appears broken at first glance.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">How to Fix Policy and Account Issues?</h3>



<p>Start by reviewing diagnostics inside Merchant Center and account notifications inside Google Ads carefully, line by line. Fix the underlying issue, not just the surface error. Update website policies, clean up claims, and request reviews only after everything is corrected. Once trust is restored, Shopping visibility often rebounds steadily rather than instantly.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">How do bidding, budget, and auction signals quietly kill ad visibility?</h2>



<p>When feeds are clean and policies are in check, yet Google Shopping ads not showing is still the reality, the problem often comes down to auction competitiveness. Google Shopping doesn’t guarantee impressions just because your products are approved. Every appearance is earned in real time, and weak bidding or budget signals can quietly push your ads out of view, making it seem like Google Shopping ads are not serving at all.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Bids That Are Too Low to Compete</h3>



<p>Shopping ads don’t run in isolation. You’re competing against other advertisers selling similar products, often with stronger performance history or higher bids. If your bids are too low, Google may technically enter you into auctions but never actually show your products. This is a very common reason Google Shopping ads not getting impressions, especially in competitive categories.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Daily Budgets That Limit Learning and Reach</h3>



<p>A budget that’s too tight doesn’t just cap spend—it limits data. When campaigns can’t gather enough signals, Google struggles to understand where and when to show your products. Over time, this can cause impressions to drop off entirely, leaving advertisers confused about why Google Shopping ads are not showing even though everything is enabled.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Smart Bidding Without Enough Data</h3>



<p>Automated bidding strategies rely on historical performance. If you switch to a smart bidding model too early, or after performance has already declined, the system may stall. This often results in Google Shopping ads not serving while Google waits for conversion signals that never arrive.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">How to Fix Bidding and Budget Issues?</h3>



<p>Start by increasing bids on priority products and giving campaigns enough budget to breathe. If you’re using automated bidding, switch back to manual temporarily to rebuild volume. Inside Google Ads, focus on impression share and auction insights to understand whether you’re losing visibility due to rank or budget. Once competitiveness improves, impressions usually follow.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Why do campaign structure, targeting, and setup mistakes block impressions?</h2>



<p>Even when your feed is clean, policies are met, and bids are competitive, Google Shopping ads not showing can still happen because of how the campaign itself is built. Shopping campaigns are less forgiving than Search. Small setup mistakes can silently block reach, making it feel like Google Shopping ads are not serving even though everything looks active.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Overly Restrictive Targeting</h3>



<p>It’s easy to narrow things too much—tight location settings, limited audiences, aggressive exclusions, or device restrictions can shrink your eligible reach to almost nothing. When this happens, Google Shopping ads not getting impressions isn’t a performance issue; it’s a visibility one. Google simply doesn’t have enough opportunities to show your products.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Product Group Segmentation Errors</h3>



<p>Poor product group structure can isolate products unintentionally. If items are placed in excluded groups, paused segments, or filtered out by attributes like brand, category, or custom labels, they won’t enter auctions at all. This is a common but overlooked reason why Google Shopping ads are not showing, especially in complex accounts.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Campaign and Account-Level Exclusions</h3>



<p>Negative keywords, brand exclusions, or account-level filters can block entire search themes without you realizing it. Over time, these restrictions stack up and reduce eligible traffic until impressions dry out completely, leading to Google Shopping ads not serving with no obvious error.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">How to Fix Structural and Targeting Issues?</h3>



<p>Audit your campaign settings carefully—locations, devices, schedules, and exclusions—one layer at a time. Review product groups inside Google Ads and confirm that products are actually included and eligible. Simplifying structure often restores impressions faster than adding complexity.</p>

<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Why does Google delay ads in new accounts?</h2>



<p>Not every case of <strong>Google Shopping ads not showing</strong> is caused by something being wrong. Sometimes, the issue is timing. Google’s systems rely heavily on data, and when there isn’t enough of it yet—or when too much has changed too quickly—ads can pause in what feels like limbo, even though nothing is technically broken. This is one of the most misunderstood reasons Google Shopping ads are not serving, especially for new or recently reworked accounts.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">New Accounts Need Trust Before Traffic</h3>



<p>Fresh Merchant Center and Ads accounts start with zero history. Google doesn’t yet know whether your products convert, whether users engage, or whether the experience is reliable. During this period, Google Shopping ads not getting impressions is common, not because of penalties, but because Google is cautious. Visibility builds gradually as trust signals accumulate.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Frequent Changes Reset the Learning Process</h3>



<p>Major changes—switching bidding strategies, restructuring campaigns, updating large parts of the feed, or changing conversion tracking—can reset learning. When this happens, impressions often drop temporarily, which leads many advertisers to ask why Google Shopping ads are not showing right after they “optimized” their account.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Limited Conversion Signals Slow Delivery</h3>



<p>Shopping campaigns, especially those using automated bidding, depend on conversion data. Without enough signals, Google may hold back impressions while it waits for clearer performance patterns. This can make it look like Google Shopping ads not serving, when in reality the system is waiting for consistency.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">How to Get Through the Learning Phase Faster?</h3>



<p>The best fix here is patience paired with stability. Avoid constant changes, keep bids and budgets steady, and let campaigns run long enough to collect data. Monitor status messages inside Google Ads, but don’t panic at short-term drops. Once learning stabilizes, impressions usually return on their own.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">How do tracking, conversion setup, and signal gaps hold ads back?</h2>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="683" height="1024" src="https://webappick.com/wp-content/uploads/2026/01/ChatGPT-Image-Jan-16-2026-12_33_04-PM-683x1024.webp" alt="Infographic titled “Tracking &amp; Signal Issues: Why Your Google Shopping Ads Aren’t Showing,” showing four panels: broken or missing conversion tracking (no accurate data, no optimization), incorrect conversion actions (wrong priorities and goals), tag and signal conflicts (duplicate tags and mixed signals), and how to fix tracking (audit and correct setup). <h2 class="wp-block-heading">What is the clear diagnostic path to get your Shopping ads visible again?</h2>



<p>At this point, one thing should be clear when Google Shopping ads not showing becomes a problem, it’s rarely random and almost never permanent. The real challenge isn’t fixing the issue—it’s knowing where to look first. Most advertisers lose time jumping between settings, increasing budgets, or rebuilding campaigns, without addressing the actual blocker that’s causing Google Shopping ads not serving in the first place.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Why Guessing Makes the Problem Worse?</h3>



<p>Shopping Ads work as a connected system. Feed quality, policy trust, bidding strength, campaign structure, learning status, and tracking signals all influence each other. If you change too many things at once, Google loses clarity, and Google Shopping ads not getting impressions can drag on longer than necessary. That’s why random “fixes” often backfire.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">What Are The Right Way to Troubleshoot (In Order)?</h3>



<p>Instead of guessing, work through a simple diagnostic flow:</p>



<ol class="wp-block-list">
<li><strong>Start with the product feed</strong> — errors, mismatches, titles, and images<br></li>



<li><strong>Check policy and account health</strong> — not just approvals, but trust signals<br></li>



<li><strong>Review bids and budgets</strong> — are you actually competitive?<br></li>



<li><strong>Audit campaign structure and exclusions</strong> — are products truly eligible?<br></li>



<li><strong>Account for learning and recent changes</strong> — did you reset the system?<br></li>



<li><strong>Verify tracking and conversions</strong> — are signals clean and reliable?<br></li>
</ol>



<p>Following this order prevents you from fixing the wrong thing while the real issue remains untouched. It also explains why Google Shopping ads are not showing even after multiple “optimizations.”</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="683" height="1024" src="https://webappick.com/wp-content/uploads/2026/01/ChatGPT-Image-Jan-16-2026-11_37_00-AM-683x1024.webp" alt="Infographic titled “The Right Way to Troubleshoot (In Order)” outlining a step-by-step diagnostic flow for Google Shopping Ads: start with the product feed (errors, mismatches, titles, images), check policy and account health, review bids and budgets, audit campaign structure, account for learning and recent changes, and verify tracking and conversions. <h2 class="wp-block-heading">The Big Picture</h2>



<p>Shopping Ads reward clarity and consistency. When Google understands your products, trusts your data, and sees clean performance signals, visibility follows. If impressions drop, it’s simply feedback that something in that chain needs attention—not that the system is broken. Fix the fundamentals, move methodically, and Google Shopping ads not showing turns from a frustrating mystery into a solvable, repeatable process.</p>

to Add Inventory in Google Merchant Center: 4 Expert Tips for Better Performance";}s:7:"content";O:8:"stdClass":2:{s:8:"rendered";s:18273:"
<p>To add inventory in <strong><a href="https://webappick.com/what-is-google-merchant-center-how-does-it-operate/">Google Merchant Center</a></strong>, inventory data is managed through your product data sources.</p>



<p>For <strong>in-store products</strong>, inventory is provided using <strong>local inventory feeds</strong>, which are configured under <strong>Settings → Data sources → Supplemental sources</strong>. </p>



<p>You can supply this data by connecting a supported provider, uploading a feed file (via HTTPS, SFTP, or manual upload), or enabling automatic inventory reporting from your website, where supported, to streamline how you Add Inventory in Google Merchant Center.</p>



<p>A primary product feed must already be active, and your website must allow <a href="https://developers.google.com/crawling/docs/crawlers-fetchers/overview-google-crawlers" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Google crawlers</a> to access product and availability data.</p>



<p>For <strong>online-only products</strong>, inventory is handled directly within the <strong>primary product feed</strong>, which must be correctly configured and submitted through <strong>Product sources</strong>. Availability and pricing in this feed determine how products appear across Google surfaces.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">What Does “Inventory” Mean in Google Merchant Center?</h2>



<p>In Google Merchant Center, inventory is not a single feature. To Add Inventory in Google Merchant Center, it is handled through three distinct but related mechanisms:</p>



<ol class="wp-block-list">
<li><strong>Primary product data<br></strong>Availability and price are included directly in your main product feed.</li>



<li><strong>Local inventory (supplemental)<br></strong>Store-level stock and price overrides are used for in-store availability and pickup experiences.</li>



<li><strong>Inventory updates via API<br></strong>Programmatic updates for availability, quantity, and price at scale.<br></li>
</ol>



<p>Google treats <strong>inventory accuracy as a policy requirement</strong>, not just a performance signal.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Ways to Add Inventory to Merchant Center</h2>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1024" height="683" src="https://webappick.com/wp-content/uploads/2026/01/ChatGPT-Image-Mar-27-2026-06_13_53-PM-1024x683.png" alt="Add Inventory in Google Merchant Center" class="wp-image-153461" title="" srcset="https://webappick.com/wp-content/uploads/2026/01/ChatGPT-Image-Mar-27-2026-06_13_53-PM-1024x683.png 1024w, https://webappick.com/wp-content/uploads/2026/01/ChatGPT-Image-Mar-27-2026-06_13_53-PM-300x200.png 300w, https://webappick.com/wp-content/uploads/2026/01/ChatGPT-Image-Mar-27-2026-06_13_53-PM-768x512.png 768w, https://webappick.com/wp-content/uploads/2026/01/ChatGPT-Image-Mar-27-2026-06_13_53-PM-570x380.png 570w, https://webappick.com/wp-content/uploads/2026/01/ChatGPT-Image-Mar-27-2026-06_13_53-PM.png 1536w" sizes="(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px" /></figure>



<p>You have several methods to Add Inventory in Google Merchant Center, depending on your setup and business needs:</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading"><strong>a) Product Feed (Primary)</strong></h3>



<p>This is the standard way to upload inventory data along with all product attributes. You include availability, price, and stock-related attributes in your product feed file.</p>



<p>This gets consumed as part of your main product catalog.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading"><strong>b) Manual Entry (Product Editor)</strong></h3>



<p>For a few products or one-off updates:</p>



<ol class="wp-block-list">
<li>Go to <em>Products</em> → <em>All products</em></li>



<li>Click <strong>Add products</strong></li>



<li>Choose “Add new products one by one”</li>



<li>Fill in availability and other required fields</li>



<li>Save the product<br></li>
</ol>



<p>Note: This is <strong>not practical for large catalogs</strong>.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading"><strong>c) Automated Online Store Sync</strong></h3>



<p>If you use Shopify, BigCommerce, or similar:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li>Connect your platform in Merchant Center</li>



<li>The system will automatically fetch product and inventory data</li>



<li>Changes on your store reflect in Merchant Center within ~24–48 hours</li>
</ul>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading"><strong>d) Local Inventory Feed (Supplemental)</strong></h3>



<p>This is required if you have brick-and-mortar store stock and want to Add Inventory in Google Merchant Center, enabling Google to show in-store availability or activate Local Inventory Ads.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Step-by-Step: Adding Local Inventory in Merchant Center</h2>



<p>Local inventory feeds help you Add Inventory in Google Merchant Center by providing per-store stock and availability details (e.g., store A has 10 units, store B has 3 units).</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Prerequisites</h3>



<p>✔ A Merchant Center account<br>✔ Primary product feed already set up<br>✔ Store locations configured in GMC<br>✔ Optional: Google Business Profile linked (recommended for local programs)</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Step 1: Log in and Navigate to Data Sources</h3>



<ol class="wp-block-list">
<li>Sign in to <strong><a href="https://webappick.com/google-shopping-free-listings/">Google Merchant Center</a></strong>.<br></li>



<li>Click <strong>Settings</strong> → <strong>Data Sources</strong>.<br></li>



<li>Select <strong>Product sources</strong> → <strong>Supplemental sources</strong>.</li>
</ol>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Step 2: Add Local Inventory</h3>



<p>Click <strong><a href="https://webappick.com/generate-google-local-inventory-feed/">Add local inventory</a></strong>.<br>Choose how you want to send the local inventory feed:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li><strong>Enter a link to your file (HTTP/HTTPS/SFTP)</strong></li>



<li><strong>Upload a file from your computer</strong></li>



<li><strong>Schedule SFTP or Google Cloud Storage fetch</strong></li>
</ul>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Step 3: Define Language and Feed Labels</h3>



<p>After selecting the input method:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li>Choose the **<strong>language</strong>**</li>



<li>Assign a **<strong>feed label</strong>**&nbsp;&nbsp;</li>
</ul>



<p><strong>Note:</strong> To properly Add Inventory in Google Merchant Center, the label must match the language and feed label used in your product feed to ensure local inventory is linked correctly.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Step 4: Schedule Fetch and Finish</h3>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li>If using a URL, set the **fetch schedule** (default ~24 hrs).&nbsp;&nbsp;</li>



<li>Save the feed.&nbsp;&nbsp;</li>



<li>Google will start parsing inventory data; global sync may take up to 24 hours initially. :contentReference[oaicite:14]{index=14}</li>
</ul>



<p>Below are<strong> example CSV + XML schemas</strong> you can upload as a <strong>Local inventory data source</strong> in Merchant Center, and<strong> a Python script</strong> that automates store-level inventory updates via the <strong>Content API for Shopping v2.1</strong>.</p>



<p>All attribute names and values must align with Google Merchant Center Help and the official API reference when you Add Inventory in Google Merchant Center.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">1) <strong>Local Inventory Feed — CSV schema + examples (Merchant Center upload)</strong></h2>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading"><strong>Required columns (minimum viable local inventory feed)</strong></h3>



<p>Google requires these fields for local inventory data:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li>id</li>



<li>store_code</li>



<li>availability</li>
</ul>



<p>Availability supported values include: in_stock, limited_availability, on_display_to_order, out_of_stock.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading"><strong>Recommended columns (common in production)</strong></h3>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li>price (store-specific override)</li>



<li>sale_price</li>



<li>sale_price_effective_date</li>



<li>quantity (optional; if provided alongside availability, Google may classify based on the lower signal)</li>



<li>Store pickup: pickup_method (optional since Sep 1, 2024), pickup_sla (required when using store pickup)</li>



<li>Local delivery: local_shipping_label</li>
</ul>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading"><strong>CSV template (schema)</strong></h2>



<pre class="wp-block-code"><code>id,store_code,availability,quantity,price,sale_price,sale_price_effective_date,pickup_method,pickup_sla,local_shipping_label</code></pre>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">CSV example (two stores, one SKU)</h2>



<pre class="wp-block-code"><code>id,store_code,availability,quantity,price,sale_price,sale_price_effective_date,pickup_method,pickup_sla,local_shipping_label
SKU-10001,STORE-001,in_stock,12,149.00 USD,129.00 USD,2026-01-01T00:00Z/2026-01-07T23:59Z,buy,same day,Sameday
SKU-10001,STORE-002,limited_availability,2,149.00 USD,,,reserve,next day,
</code></pre>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">2) Local Inventory Feed — XML schema + example (RSS 2.0)</h2>



<p>Merchant Center supports XML feeds using an RSS 2.0 structure with the <code>g:</code> namespace.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">XML example</h3>



<pre class="wp-block-code"><code>&lt;?xml version="1.0" encoding="UTF-8"?&gt;
&lt;rss version="2.0" xmlns:g="http://base.google.com/ns/1.0"&gt;
  &lt;channel&gt;
    &lt;title&gt;Local Inventory Feed&lt;/title&gt;
    &lt;link&gt;https://example.com&lt;/link&gt;
    &lt;description&gt;Store-level inventory updates&lt;/description&gt;

    &lt;item&gt;
      &lt;g:id&gt;SKU-10001&lt;/g:id&gt;
      &lt;g:store_code&gt;STORE-001&lt;/g:store_code&gt;
      &lt;g:availability&gt;in_stock&lt;/g:availability&gt;
      &lt;g:quantity&gt;12&lt;/g:quantity&gt;
      &lt;g:price&gt;149.00 USD&lt;/g:price&gt;
      &lt;g:sale_price&gt;129.00 USD&lt;/g:sale_price&gt;
      &lt;g:sale_price_effective_date&gt;2026-01-01T00:00Z/2026-01-07T23:59Z&lt;/g:sale_price_effective_date&gt;
      &lt;g:pickup_method&gt;buy&lt;/g:pickup_method&gt;
      &lt;g:pickup_sla&gt;same day&lt;/g:pickup_sla&gt;
      &lt;g:local_shipping_label&gt;Sameday&lt;/g:local_shipping_label&gt;
    &lt;/item&gt;

    &lt;item&gt;
      &lt;g:id&gt;SKU-10001&lt;/g:id&gt;
      &lt;g:store_code&gt;STORE-002&lt;/g:store_code&gt;
      &lt;g:availability&gt;limited_availability&lt;/g:availability&gt;
      &lt;g:quantity&gt;2&lt;/g:quantity&gt;
      &lt;g:price&gt;149.00 USD&lt;/g:price&gt;
      &lt;g:pickup_method&gt;reserve&lt;/g:pickup_method&gt;
      &lt;g:pickup_sla&gt;next day&lt;/g:pickup_sla&gt;
    &lt;/item&gt;

  &lt;/channel&gt;
&lt;/rss&gt;
</code></pre>



<p>The required attributes for local inventory remain <code>id</code>, <code>store_code</code>, and <code>availability</code>.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">3) Automating store inventory updates via the Content API (localinventory.custombatch)</h2>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">What you can update via API</h3>



<p>The local inventory service supports updates per store using:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li>Required: <code>storeCode</code> + <code>productId</code> (productId in URL / request entry)</li>



<li>Optional: <code>quantity</code>, <code>price</code>, <code>salePrice</code>, <code>salePriceEffectiveDate</code>, <code>availability</code>, <code>pickupMethod</code>, <code>pickupSla</code>, <code>instoreProductLocation</code> .</li>
</ul>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading">Endpoint + auth scope (v2.1)</h3>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li><code>POST https://shoppingcontent.googleapis.com/content/v2.1/localinventory/batch</code></li>



<li>OAuth scope: <code>https://www.googleapis.com/auth/content</code></li>
</ul>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">4) Python script (reads CSV → pushes updates with localinventory.custombatch)</h2>



<p>This script:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li>Reads a CSV shaped like the template above</li>



<li>Builds batch entries</li>



<li>Sends <code>localinventory.custombatch</code> updates</li>
</ul>



<blockquote class="wp-block-quote is-layout-flow wp-block-quote-is-layout-flow">
<p>You must already be enrolled in a local program and have local products set up; local inventory attaches to <strong>existing local products</strong>.</p>
</blockquote>



<pre class="wp-block-code"><code>import csv
import json
import os
import requests
from google.oauth2 import service_account
from google.auth.transport.requests import Request

# ----------------------------
# Config
# ----------------------------
SERVICE_ACCOUNT_JSON = os.environ.get("GOOGLE_APPLICATION_CREDENTIALS", "service-account.json")
MERCHANT_ID = os.environ.get("GMC_MERCHANT_ID")  # e.g., "123456789"
CSV_PATH = os.environ.get("LOCAL_INVENTORY_CSV", "local_inventory.csv")

SCOPE = "https://www.googleapis.com/auth/content"
BATCH_ENDPOINT = "https://shoppingcontent.googleapis.com/content/v2.1/localinventory/batch"


def get_access_token() -&gt; str:
    """
    Uses a service account to obtain an access token.
    The service account must be granted access to your Merchant Center account.
    """
    creds = service_account.Credentials.from_service_account_file(
        SERVICE_ACCOUNT_JSON,
        scopes=&#091;SCOPE],
    )
    creds.refresh(Request())
    return creds.token


def parse_money(value: str):
    """
    Converts '149.00 USD' -&gt; {'value': '149.00', 'currency': 'USD'}
    """
    if not value:
        return None
    parts = value.strip().split()
    if len(parts) != 2:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid money format: {value!r} (expected 'NUMBER CURRENCY')")
    amount, currency = parts
    return {"value": amount, "currency": currency}


def build_batch_entries(csv_path: str, merchant_id: str):
    entries = &#091;]
    batch_id = 1

    with open(csv_path, newline="", encoding="utf-8") as f:
        reader = csv.DictReader(f)
        for row in reader:
            product_id = row&#091;"id"].strip()
            store_code = row&#091;"store_code"].strip()
            availability = (row.get("availability") or "").strip()
            quantity_raw = (row.get("quantity") or "").strip()
            price_raw = (row.get("price") or "").strip()
            sale_price_raw = (row.get("sale_price") or "").strip()
            sale_price_effective_date = (row.get("sale_price_effective_date") or "").strip()
            pickup_method = (row.get("pickup_method") or "").strip()
            pickup_sla = (row.get("pickup_sla") or "").strip()
            # local_shipping_label is not a field on the API localInventory object in v2.1;
            # it’s typically provided via feed uploads, not the localinventory API call.

            local_inventory = {"storeCode": store_code}

            # Optional fields
            if availability:
                local_inventory&#091;"availability"] = availability
            if quantity_raw:
                local_inventory&#091;"quantity"] = int(quantity_raw)
            if price_raw:
                local_inventory&#091;"price"] = parse_money(price_raw)
            if sale_price_raw:
                local_inventory&#091;"salePrice"] = parse_money(sale_price_raw)
            if sale_price_effective_date:
                local_inventory&#091;"salePriceEffectiveDate"] = sale_price_effective_date
            if pickup_method:
                local_inventory&#091;"pickupMethod"] = pickup_method
            if pickup_sla:
                local_inventory&#091;"pickupSla"] = pickup_sla

            entries.append(
                {
                    "batchId": batch_id,
                    "merchantId": str(merchant_id),
                    "method": "insert",
                    "productId": product_id,
                    "localInventory": local_inventory,
                }
            )
            batch_id += 1

    return entries


def push_custombatch(access_token: str, entries):
    headers = {
        "Authorization": f"Bearer {access_token}",
        "Content-Type": "application/json",
    }
    payload = {"entries": entries}

    resp = requests.post(BATCH_ENDPOINT, headers=headers, data=json.dumps(payload), timeout=60)
    resp.raise_for_status()
    return resp.json()


def main():
    if not MERCHANT_ID:
        raise RuntimeError("Set GMC_MERCHANT_ID env var to your Merchant Center merchantId.")

    token = get_access_token()
    entries = build_batch_entries(CSV_PATH, MERCHANT_ID)

    # Tip: Google APIs often have practical batch-size limits; chunk if your file is large.
    result = push_custombatch(token, entries)

    # Print batch errors (if any)
    for entry in result.get("entries", &#091;]):
        if "errors" in entry:
            print("ERROR:", json.dumps(entry&#091;"errors"], indent=2))
        else:
            print("OK batchId:", entry.get("batchId"))

    print("Done.")


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
</code></pre>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Why “method”: &#8220;insert&#8221; for updates?</h2>



<p><code>localinventory.custombatch</code> entries accept <code>method: "insert"</code> and the API updates local inventory for the product/store in that entry.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading">Final Thoughts</h2>



<p>Inventory management in <strong><a href="https://webappick.com/how-to-upload-products-to-google-merchant-center/">Google Merchant Center</a></strong> should be aligned with your retail model. Online-only merchants should manage inventory directly through their primary product feeds, while omnichannel retailers must implement local inventory feeds to accurately represent in-store availability. </p>



<p>Enterprises with large catalogs or high-frequency inventory changes should rely on the Content API to enable scalable, near-real-time updates for efficient Add Inventory in Google Merchant Center workflows.</p>



<p>A correctly implemented inventory setup is critical, as it directly affects product eligibility, ad delivery, local discovery across Google surfaces, and the overall trust and health of your Merchant Center account.</p>

For in-store products, inventory is provided using local inventory feeds, which are configured under Settings → Data sources → Supplemental sources. You can supply this data by connecting a supported provider, uploading a feed file (via HTTPS, SFTP, or [&hellip;]</p>
